"23:29:00", "isDuplicate": false, "body": "The avian influenza, or bird flu, is an infectious disease caused by type A strains of the influenza virus.\n\nThe various strains of avian flu may cause a mild form of the disease in birds, characterized by disheveled feathers, reduced egg production and mild effects on their respiratory system.\n\nThey may also cause a rare deadly form, which does not only affect respiratory tracts but also attacks multiple organs and tissues in birds.\n\nAvian flu is transmitted from one area to another by the movement of birds, people with contaminated clothing, contaminated farm equipment and chicken feed, among others.\n\nThe H5N1 strain has posed threats to human health since its first reported human infection in 1997 during an outbreak in Hong Kong.\n\nThis strain is transmitted to humans through contact with infected birds. H5N1 is also spread through bird feces, which, when dried and pulverized, may be inhaled by humans.\n\nSince its reemergence in 2003, this avian virus has spread from Asia to Europe and Africa, and has become entrenched in poultry populations in some countries.\n\nThere were 859 reported cases of human H5N1 virus infection from 16 countries from 2003 to 2017, according to the World Health Organization (WHO). WHO said 453 cases were fatal.\n\nThe first recorded bird flu case in the Philippines was in July 2005 in a small farm in Calumpit, Bulacan. It was found to be a \"low-pathogen\" strain and not harmful to humans.\n\nThe country has since implemented precautionary measures, such as strict bans on poultry imports from affected countries and surveillance of migratory birds.\n\nThe death of an overseas Filipino worker (OFW) after his return from China in 2015 was considered a possible case of avian flu due to his travel history, exposure to live poultry, the symptoms he showed and the quick progress of his condition.\n\nAfter considering the danger of shedding the virus into the environment if an autopsy was performed, the Department of Health instead conducted a fully guided biopsy on the remains of the victim. The results were \"inconclusive,\" then Health Secretary Janette Garin said.\n\n\"The body was cremated, hence, this case has been declared closed,\" she said, adding that there was no other way to safely confirm if the OFW had indeed died of bird flu.\n\nA low pathogenic avian influenza virus, the H7N9 virus subtype, has been infecting birds and people in China. The strain first infected three humans in China in March 2013. In January this year, as many as 79 people have died from H7N9 in China bringing the total human death toll from the H7N9 strain to 100 since October last year.\n\nOutbreaks across Europe and Asia have been ongoing since late last year, leading to mass culling of poultry in many countries. Strains currently documented as circulating in birds include H5N8 in many parts of Europe as well as in Kuwait, Egypt and elsewhere, and H5N1 in Bangladesh and India. -- INQUIRER RESEARCH", "timeCrawl": "23:29:00", "sim": 0.5137255191802979, "extractedDates": [{"date": "2005-07-", "textStart": 1317, "amb": false, "textEnd": 1326}, {"date": "2013-03-", "textStart": 2445, "amb": false, "textEnd": 2455}], "location": null, "originalArticle": null, "date": "2017-08-11", "id": "8718991", "categories": [{"uri": "dmoz/Health/Conditions_and_Diseases/Infectious_Diseases", "label": "dmoz/Health/Conditions and Diseases/Infectious Diseases", "id": 625, "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Recreation/Birding", "label": "dmoz/Recreation/Birding", "id": 1163, "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Recreation/Birding/North_America", "label": "dmoz/Recreation/Birding/North America", "id": 1164, "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Recreation/Birding/Europe", "label": "dmoz/Recreation/Birding/Europe", "id": 1165, "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Health/Animal/Birds", "label": "dmoz/Health/Animal/Birds", "id": 1224, "wgt": 100}], "details": {}}

"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rappler", "label": {"eng": "Rappler"}, "type": "org", "id": "540628", "score": 3}], "time": "00:44:00", "isDuplicate": false, "body": "BIRD FLU. Department of Agriculture Secretary Manny Pinol (C), Dr Arlene Vytiaco (R), and Dr Celia Carlos of RITM, (left) confirm cases of avian influenza subtype H5 on Friday, August 11, at a press conference in Quezon City. Photo by Darren Langit/Rappler\n\nMANILA, Philippines - The Department of Agriculture (DA) said on Friday, August 11, that there were no reports of animal-to-human transmission of the avian influenza or bird flu yet in the Philippines.\n\nAgriculture Secretary Emmanuel Pi\u00f1ol on Friday confirmed reports of an outbreak of avian influenza type A subtype H5 affecting 6 farms in the town of San Luis, Pampanga.\n\n\"'Yung type ng virus na 'to, wala pa tayong reported na animal-to-human transmission (This type of virus, we have no reports yet of animal-to-human transmission),\" the secretary told reporters during Friday's press briefing.\n\nHe added: \"Healthy naman 'yung mga workers in the area, wala naman silang [team] napansin, kasi pumasok sila sa area e, wala naman silang napansin na nagkasakit doon sa mga farms na pinuntahan nila.\"\n\n(The workers in the area are healthy, our team did not observe anything, because they entered the area, and they did not observe anyone who got sick in those farms they visited.)\n\nThe Department of Health (DOH) already sent a team to assess the workers in the areas affected.\n\n\"The DOH has stepped-up the human flu like-illness surveillance since the reported human influenza outbreaks in Hong Kong and India few months back and will now look for human cases who may have been exposed to avian flu strain in affected areas,\" the DOH said in a statement.\n\nCelia Carlos of the Research Institute for Tropical Medicine (RITM) said they have already advised workers and their contacts to immediately report to the DOH if they experience influenza-like symptoms, such as fever, muscle pain, colds, conjunctivitis, or diarrhea.\n\nCarlos said that according to the World Health Organization, avian influenza in humans are usually H5N1 or H5N7.\n\n\"Avian [influenza] is not readily transmissible from animals to humans. In other words, the transmission risk is low, although once the infection is acquired, the mortality is high; according to reports, it's about 50% on the average,\" she explained.\n\nShe added: \"It is a concern, and the exposed persons which develop symptoms should therefore be assessed, especially those in the extremes of age - below 2 years old and above 50 years old - and those with concomitant medical problems.\"\n\nArlene Vytiaco of the Bureau of Animal Industry (BAI) said tests for the N1 component yielded negative results.\n\n\"So next thing to do is isu-submit sa reference lab sa Australia, kasi dalawa po 'yung sub-types natin na transmissible to man: H5N1 at H5N6. So ipapadala natin sa Australia to verify kung ano 'yung N component,\" Vytiaco explained.\n\n(So next thing to do is to submit to the reference laboratory in Australia, because there are two sub-types that are transmissible to man: H5N1 and H5N6. So we'll send this to Australia to verify what the N component is.)\n\nThe BAI is already in the process of sending samples for further testing to the Australian Animal Health Laboratory, a World Organization for Animal reference laboratory for avian influenza.\n\nThe health department said RITM has the capacity to confirm cases, and that it will coordinate with other concerned agencies to prevent human cases.\n\n\"The DOH has supply of anti-flu medication and commodities whenever regional health offices and hospitals will require these. In the interim, all health providers should observe respiratory precautions when taking care of patients with flu or flu-like illness. Properly cooked chicken remains safe to eat,\" the department added. - Rappler.com", "timeCrawl": "00:44:00", "sim": 0.4470588266849518, "extractedDates": [{"imp": true, "freq": 2, "amb": false, "textEnd": 186, "date": "2017-08-11", "textStart": 177}], "location": null, "originalArticle": null, "date": "2017-08-12", "id": "8725933", "categories": [{"uri": "dmoz/Society/Issues/Animal_Welfare", "label": "dmoz/Society/Issues/Animal Welfare", "id": 532, "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Health/Conditions_and_Diseases/Infectious_Diseases", "label": "dmoz/Health/Conditions and Diseases/Infectious Diseases", "id": 625, "wgt": 100}, {"uri": "dmoz/Health/Animal/Birds", "label": "dmoz/Health/Animal/Birds", "id": 1224, "wgt": 100}], "details": {}}

yet highly pathogenic or deadly, as there are still no reported cases of human infection, although the World Health Organization said that human infection is primarily acquired through direct contact with infected animals.\n\nBut the DOH cautioned that residents should not go near infected farms or handle birds from areas that have been infected by Avian Influenza.\n\nEarlier, Health Assistant avian flu can be acquired by eating properly cooked eggs or poultry.\n\nIt could be recalled that 37,000 birds have died from Avian Influenza subtype H5 in what has been declared as the first bird flu
